What Is Kalam?
advanced
10 min
No Definition from Two Negatives: Existence is Self-Evident
Commentator argues that definitions cannot consist of two purely negative concepts, establishing existence as self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a definition cannot be composed solely of negative concepts and why existence must be a self-evident positive notion.

Core Idea
Every valid definition must ultimately rest on a positive, self-evident concept.

In this passage, the commentator defends the doctrine that the concept of existence is self-evident. He addresses objections that attempt to define existence through negation, showing that such definitions are circular or impossible. The key principle is that pure negatives cannot serve as foundations for definitions—some positive content is always presupposed. This lesson guides you through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the dense commentary style and the sequence of objections and replies.
The passage contains multiple layers: author's statement, objections, and commentator's responses.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the assertion that existence is self-evident and the principle that a definition cannot be composed of two purely negative concepts.
Look for the phrase 'لا تعريف عن مفهومين سلبيين'.
3
Trace the argument against two negatives
Follow the commentator's reasoning: if we take two negatives as parts of a definition, the definiendum is still a negation relative to a positive, so a positive concept is required.
The example given: 'The Necessary is neither corporeal nor inhering'.
4
Understand the three objections to the alternative interpretation
The commentator refutes a reinterpretation of the author's intent with three points. Note each point and why it undermines the reinterpretation.
The three points are marked by 'أما أولاً', 'أما ثانياً', 'أما ثالثاً'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
قوله : ( بأن الكلام إلخ حيث صرح بأن تصور وجودي بديهي فالتنزل عنه هو القول بأن تصوره كسبي، وما قيل : من أنه يمكن أن يحمل التصور بمعنى العلم مطلقاً فيؤول إلى أن العلم بوجودي بديهي، ويكون محتملاً للمعنيين ككلام الإمام فبعيد غاية البعد، إذ العدول عن العلم مع وقوعه في كلام الإمام الذي هو مأخذ هذا الوجه إلى لفظ التصور المتبادر منه خلاف المقصود مما لا يجترى عليه عاقل .
قوله : ( سلبيين) أي مفهومين تكون ماهيتهما مجرد السلب من غير إضافة إلى شيء.
قوله : (لا يعقل إلخ ) لأنه رفع لثبوت شيء في نفسه أو لغيره .
على هذا حيث قال: وجوابه أنا لا نسلم أن وجودي حقيقته متصورة بالبديهة، نعم أنا موجود تصديق بديهي إلخ، فإنه جعل في هذا الكلام تصور وجودي مقابلا للتصديق الذي هو أنا ،موجود فلو حمل كلامه السابق على الاحتمال المذكور لاختل تقرير الجواب هذا، وقد أجاب بعض الأفاضل عن الإشكال المذكور بوجه آخر حيث قال : واعلم أن الشارح قد حمل كلام المصنف هذا على أنه مسوق لا كتساب تصور وجودي فحكم باتجاه الإشكال ولا يخفى أن مراد المصنف هاهنا تصوير طريق آخر لبداهة تصور الوجود وحاصله وإن سلمنا أن تصور الوجود كسبي لكن يجب انتهاؤه إلى كاسب وجود ضروري، فيثبت المطلوب ثم أراد أن ينتقل من طريق الموصل التصوري إلى الموصل التصديقي لا على معنى أنه يكتسب به تصور وجودي، بل من حيث أنه موصل دعوى ما فقال : أو نقول إلخ فالزم هاهنا أيضاً، وجوداً متصوراً بالبداهة فيثبت المطلوب بهذا الطريق أيضاً هذا كلامه وأنت خبير بأن سياق الكلام يأبى عن هذا التوجيه أما أولاً فلان الواو في قوله ولا دليل مانع عنه عند من له أدنى دربة في صناعة التركيب، إذ الوجه أن يقول : أونقول لا دليل عن سالبتين وأما ثانيا فلانه لو قصد ذلك لكفى أن يقول لا شك في وجود قضية بديهية موجبة، وأما ثالثاً فلأن هذا الوجه حينئذ دليل مستقل، فالوجه أن يعد دليلاً ثانياً، وتصير به الوجوه أربعة لا ثلاثة كما قرره المصنف .
قوله: (فلعله أراد كما أنه إلخ) قيل : لا حاجة إليه فإنا لا نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان اكتسابه بدليل ولا دليل عن سالبتين إلخ، بل نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان العلم بكسبيته بدليل مركب من مقدمتين إحداهما لإيجابها تشتمل على العلم بوجود خاص بالبداهة وفيه بحث، إذ لا نسلم الملازمة حينئذ فإن كسبية شيء لا تستلزم كسبية العلم بكسبيته، بل الأقرب حينئذ بداهة هذا العلم وإن جاز كسبيته كما حققناه في مباحث العلم.
قوله كذلك لا تعريف عن مفهومين (سلبيين فإن قلت : يجوز أن يقال : الواجب لا متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز قلت : إن اعتبر جزءا التعريف معدولتين يدخل متعلق السلب التعريف وبه يتم المطلوب، وإن أخذا سالبتين فلا شك أن المعرف هو السلب المضاف من حيث
بد في المعرف من مفهوم وجودي إما ضروري أو منته إليه، فيكون العلم بوجوده ضرورياً ، فكذا الوجود المطلق في ضمنه
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-existence. His statement: (that the speech etc.) where he explicitly stated that the conceptualization of my existence is self-evident, so descending from that is the statement that its conceptualization is acquired. And what was said: that 'tasawwur' can be taken in the sense of knowledge absolutely, so it would mean that knowledge of my existence is self-evident, and it would be possible for two meanings like the Imām's speech — that is very far-fetched, because turning away from 'knowledge' when it occurs in the Imam's speech, which is the source of this aspect, to the word 'tasawwur' which otherwise indicates something contrary to the intended meaning, is something no rational person would dare do. His statement: (two negative [concepts]) i.e., two concepts whose quiddity is mere negation without addition to anything. His statement: (it is not intelligible etc.) because it is the removal of the affirmation of something in itself or of something else. On this basis, where he said: 'The answer is that we do not grant that the reality of my existence is conceptualized by self-evidence; rather, that I exist is a self-evident assent, etc.,' for he made in this speech the conceptualization of my existence opposite to the assent which is 'I exist.' So if his earlier speech were interpreted according to the mentioned probability, the formulation of this answer would be spoiled. And some virtuous scholars have answered the mentioned difficulty with another approach, saying: Know that the Commentator interpreted the author's speech as being directed at acquiring a conceptualization of my existence, so he judged that the difficulty arises. And it is not hidden that the author's intent here is to depict another path for the self-evidence of the conceptualization of existence. Its summary: even if we grant that the conceptualization of existence is acquired, it must ultimately terminate in an existence that is necessary, so the required is established. Then he intended to move from the conceptual conveyer to the assenting conveyer, not meaning that an existential conceptualization is acquired thereby, but insofar as it conveys a claim. So he said: 'Or we say, etc.' So then here too, an existence conceptualized by self-evidence is necessary, so the required is established by this path also. This is his speech. And you know that the context of the speech refuses this interpretation. First: because the waw in his statement 'and no proof prevents' is problematic for anyone with the least experience in the art of composition, for the correct expression is 'or we say no proof from two negatives.' Second: because if he intended that, it would suffice to say 'there is no doubt about the existence of a self-evident affirmative proposition.' Third: because this aspect would then be an independent proof, so it should be counted as a second proof, making the aspects four, not three, as the author formulated. His statement: (perhaps he intended just as etc.) It was said: There is no need for it, for we do not say: If it were acquired, its acquisition would be by a proof, and there is no proof from two negatives, etc. Rather we say: If it were acquired, the knowledge of its being acquired would be by a compound proof from two premises, one of which contains knowledge of a specific existence by self-evidence, and there is discussion therein, for we do not grant the implication then, because the acquiredness of a thing does not entail the acquiredness of the knowledge of its acquiredness; rather, the closest then is the self-evidence of this knowledge, even if its acquiredness is possible, as we have verified in the investigations of knowledge. His statement likewise: 'No definition from two negative concepts.' For if you say: It is permissible to say: The Necessary is neither corporeal nor inhering in a corporeal. I say: If the two parts of the definition are considered as modified [affirmative by negation], then the object of the negation enters the definition and the required is complete. If they are taken as two negations, then there is no doubt that the definiendum is the negated thing from the aspect that it is additional, so the positive addition enters. Then it is necessary in the definiendum [there must be] a positive concept, either necessary or terminating to it, so the knowledge of its existence is necessary. Thus the absolute existence within it [is also necessary].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a commentary and objection-answer sequence regarding the definition of existence. The commentator begins by clarifying that the author (al-Mawaqif) explicitly states that the conceptualization (tasawwur) of one's own existence is self-evident (badīhī). A detractor might argue that 'tasawwur' could be interpreted as 'knowledge' in general, leading to the claim that knowledge of existence is self-evident. The commentator dismisses this as far-fetched, because the Imam (al-Rāzī) used 'ilm in the original text, and changing it to 'tasawwur' would distort the intended meaning. Next, the commentator explains 'salbiyyayn' as two concepts whose quiddity (māhiyya) is pure negation without any positive content. He then discusses the statement that negation is not intelligible except in relation to affirmation. The commentary then addresses an attempt by some scholars to reinterpret the author's intent as merely presenting an alternative path rather than acquiring the concept of existence. The commentator refutes this reinterpretation with three points: (1) the wording 'wa lā dalīl' should be 'aw naqūl lā dalīl'; (2) if that were intended, it would suffice to say 'there is no doubt about a self-evident affirmative proposition'; (3) it would make the argument an independent proof, contradicting the author's classification of three arguments. The commentator then addresses a further objection that the argument about acquiredness is unnecessary, and he explains the proper formulation: if existence were acquired, the knowledge of its acquiredness would require a proof, but that knowledge is itself self-evident. Finally, the passage discusses the principle that a definition cannot be composed of two purely negative concepts. Using the example of 'the Necessary is neither corporeal nor inhering', the commentator shows that if the terms are taken as modified affirmatives (the negation of a positive attribute), the definition works; if taken as pure negations, the definiendum is still the negation considered as a relative concept, which presupposes a positive concept. Therefore, any definition must ultimately rest on a positive, self-evident concept, and absolute existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq) is such a concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a definition can be composed entirely of negative concepts without involving any positive notion.
Correction: The passage shows that even when the terms are taken as pure negations, the definiendum is still a negation relative to a positive, so a positive concept must be presupposed. Hence, a valid definition cannot consist solely of negatives.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a definition not be formed from two purely negative concepts?
A) Because negative concepts are unintelligible.
B) Because any definition requires a positive concept either necessarily or as endpoint.
C) Because the author explicitly forbids it.
D) Because negation cannot be added to other negations.

Key Takeaway
No definition can rest solely on negative concepts; a positive, self-evident concept like existence is always required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every valid definition requires a positive concept that is self-evident or terminates to one, what is the self-evident positive concept in your own life that anchors your certainty? Have you ever built your faith on mere negations—what God is not—without grounding it in the undeniable reality of existence itself? Will you commit today to rooting your understanding of God in the self-evident truth that He is, not just what He is not?
?
Do you truly believe that your own existence is self-evident? Or do you sometimes doubt it, slipping into a kind of existential skepticism that undermines your certainty about the Creator? If you cannot even be sure of your own existence, how can you be sure of His? This lesson forces you to confront that your own existence is the first evidence of the Necessary Being. Have you been avoiding this truth?
?
When you think about God, do you find yourself more comfortable describing Him by what He is not (not limited, not bodily) rather than affirming positive attributes? This lesson teaches that such an approach is ultimately incoherent without a positive concept. Are you using negations as a crutch to avoid the awe of affirming His real, positive attributes? What would change in your worship if you instead focused on His positive perfections—His power, His mercy, His self-sufficiency?

The Definition Requirement and the Objection on Intuitive Existence
Al-Jurjani presents the definition requirement and answers the objection that existence is not intuitively known in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definitions require a concept of existence and why the intuitive knowledge of existence is a belief, not a conception of essence.

Core Idea
Definitions need existence, but intuitive existence is belief, not essence-conception.

This lesson tackles a foundational point in kalam: how can we define existence itself? Al-Jurjani asserts that every definition must involve a concept of existence. But an objection arises: if existence is intuitively known, why can't we define it? The answer distinguishes between the intuitive belief that we exist and the conceptual grasp of the essence of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a definition requirement followed by an objection and its answer.
The passage uses technical terms like 'tasdiq' and 'tasawwur'.
2
Identify the definition requirement
Find where Al-Jurjani states that every definition must include a concept of existence, either intuitive or leading to the intuitive. Note that he says knowledge of existence is necessary.
This sets up the problem for defining existence itself.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims that my existence in its essence is not conceived intuitively. Instead, what is intuitive is the belief 'I exist'. Grasp why this challenges the definition requirement.
The objection denies that we have an intuitive conception of the essence of existence.
4
Grasp the distinction between tasdiq and tasawwur
The answer clarifies that the intuitive belief does not require conceiving the essence of existence, only a certain aspect. This distinction resolves the objection.
This is key: we can know we exist without knowing the quiddity of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
بد في المعرف من مفهوم وجودي إما ضروري أو منته إليه، فيكون العلم بوجوده ضرورياً ، فكذا الوجود المطلق في ضمنه ( وجوابه ) أي جواب الوجه الأول (أنا لا نسلم أن وجودي حقيقته بكنهها (متصورة بالبديهة نعم أنا موجود تصديق بديهي ) حاصل لمن لا يتصور منه كسب ( وإنه لا يستدعي تصور وجودي بالكنه، بل باعتبار ما كما أن أحد طرفيه أنا والمشار إليه بأنا حقيقته ) بكنهها ( غير بديهية )
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On Defining Existence and Non-existence. It is necessary in the definition to have a concept of existence, either necessary or ending in it, so knowledge of its existence is necessary, and thus absolute existence within it. (And its answer, i.e., the answer to the first aspect: We do not concede that my existence in its essence is conceived intuitively. Yes, I exist is an intuitive belief, obtained by one who does not acquire it from effort, and it does not require conceiving my existence in its essence, but only in a certain aspect, just as one of its two sides is 'I' and the referent of 'I' in its essence is not intuitive.)
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the initial definition requirement and the first objection against it. The author states that every definition must include a concept of existence that is either intuitively known or leads to what is intuitively known. Therefore, knowledge of the existence of the definiendum is necessary, and so absolute existence within it is also necessary. The objector says: 'We do not concede that my existence in its essence is conceived intuitively.' Rather, what is intuitive is the belief 'I exist,' which is a belief (tasdiq), not a conception (tasawwur). This belief does not require conceiving the essence of my existence; it only requires a certain aspect of it. An analogy is given: just as one of the two sides of this belief is the concept of 'I', and the referent of 'I' in its essence is not intuitive, so too the concept of existence in its essence is not intuitive. The answer thus denies the claim that the essence of existence is known intuitively, while affirming that it is known in some aspect.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
المعرف
المفهوم الوجودي
البديهي
التصديق
التصور
الكنه
الوجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because we intuitively know we exist, we must also intuitively know the essence of existence.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between the intuitive belief (tasdiq) that one exists and the conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the essence of existence. Only the belief is intuitive, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the belief 'I exist'?
A) It is an intuitive belief that requires conceiving the essence of existence.
B) It is an intuitive belief that does not require conceiving the essence of existence.
C) It is not intuitive at all.
D) It is an acquired belief.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive knowledge of existence is a belief (tasdiq), not a conceptual understanding (tasawwur) of its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I exist,' is that a genuine belief from the depths of your soul, or a thoughtless habit you repeat without awareness? What would change if you took that belief seriously?
?
Have you ever paused to contemplate the essence of your own existence beyond the daily routine? What prevents you from seeking the true meaning of 'I'?
?
This lesson distinguishes between merely believing you exist and truly understanding your essence. Which one describes your current state? What would it take to move from belief to conception?

What Is Kalam?
beginner

The Self-Evident Nature of Existence
The author defines existence and discusses the conditions for its self-evident conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which existence is considered self-evidently conceived and the implications of different interpretations of the meaning of existence.

Core Idea
The self-evidence of existence depends on whether it is an essential or accidental property.

This lesson begins by defining existence and clarifying that the debate is not about whether existence exists, but about whether we can have a full, self-evident conception of it. The author, al-Ījī, presents three possible scenarios for the nature of existence and explains the consequences for the possibility of conceiving it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's structured approach: he first states the subject, then outlines three conditional analyses.
2
Identify the three scenarios
Look for the three conditions: (1) existence as a single shared meaning essential to its particulars, (2) existence as homonymous, (3) existence as an accident. Understand what each means.
These conditions determine whether existence can be conceived self-evidently.
3
Understand the conclusion
Understand why the author says the self-evident conception only holds in the first scenario. Reflect on how this sets up the debate in kalam.
This connects to the broader discussion of the validity of theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم. وجود ولا نزاع فيه، إنما الكلام في أن تصوره بكنهه بديهي هذا إذا كان الوجود معنى واحداً مشتركاً وذاتياً لما تحته من الجزئيات، أما إذا كان مشتركاً لفظياً فليس هناك مطلق يتصور بداهة أو كسباً، وإذا كان عارضاً لأفراده لم يلزم من تصور أفراده بالكنه بداهة تصور عارضها أصلا
First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. Existence: there is no dispute about it. The discussion is only about whether its full conception (tasawwuruhu bi-kunhihi) is self-evident (badihi). This is if existence is one shared meaning and essential to its particulars. But if it is homonymous (mushtarak lafzi), then there is no absolute that can be conceived either self-evidently or by acquisition. And if it is an accident to its individuals, then it does not follow from the self-evident full conception of its individuals that the full conception of its accident is self-evident at all.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines the subject of discussion: existence. The author states that there is no dispute about existence itself; the dispute is about whether its full conception (bi-kunhihi) is self-evident (badihi). He then explains that this depends on the nature of existence. If existence is a single meaning (ma'na wahid mushtarak) and is essential (dhati) to its particulars, then its conception is self-evident. But if it is homonymous (mushtarak lafzi), then there is no absolute conception at all. And if it is an accident ('aridh) to its individuals, then even if the individuals are conceived self-evidently, the accident's full conception does not follow. This sets up the conditions for the debate.

Key Terms
وجود
عدم
تصور بالكنه
بديهي
معنى مشترك
ذاتي
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think existence is always self-evident, but the author shows it depends on whether it is essential or accidental.
Correction: The passage clarifies that self-evidence is conditional upon existence being a single essential meaning; otherwise, it is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the conception of existence by its essence (bi-kunhihi) self-evident?
A) When existence is homonymous (mushtarak lafzi)
B) When existence is a single shared meaning and essential to its particulars
C) When existence is an accident to its individuals
D) When existence is not conceived at all

Key Takeaway
The full conception of existence is self-evident only if existence is a single shared meaning essential to its particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own existence is a simple, obvious fact? After reading this passage, do you now realize that even the concept of existence requires conditions to be self-evident? How does that affect your certainty about your existence?
?
If existence is only self-evident when it is essential, then what does that imply about your existence as a contingent being? Are you comfortable with your existence being accidental? How does that shape your understanding of your dependence on the Necessary Being?
?
The author distinguishes three scenarios for existence. Which scenario do you think best describes the existence of the universe? Reflect on whether you have ever considered that the term 'existence' might be ambiguous. How would that ambiguity affect your arguments for God's existence?

Conceptual Parthood Does Not Imply Real Parthood
First objection and response, with proof analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of existence being part of the concept of 'existential' does not require that the reality of existence be part of the reality of 'existential', and how the analogy with proof supports this.

Core Idea
Concepts can be accidental, so conceptual parthood does not imply real parthood.

In this passage, the commentator addresses an objection against the definition of existence. The objection assumes that if a concept is part of another concept, the corresponding realities must also be part. Using an analogy from the logic of proof, the commentator denies this entailment and explains that inference is based on truth, not existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and denial
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection: if concept of existence is part of concept of 'existential', then reality of existence must be part of reality of 'existential'. Then see the commentator's denial and the reason: because the two concepts might be accidental to their reality.
2
Analyze the proof analogy
The commentator provides an analogy from proof: we infer from the truth of premises, not from their external existence. This illustrates that conceptual relations do not require real relations. Understand how this analogy supports the denial.
The proof can be non-existential (e.g., absence of clouds indicates absence of rain).
3
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this passage fits into the definition of existence. The commentator is showing that conceptualization suffices, not real knowledge.

يلزم من كون مفهوم الوجود جزءاً من مفهوم وجودي أن يكون حقيقة الوجود جزءاً من حقيقة وجودي لجواز أن يكون هذان المفهومان عارضين لحقيقتهما ( قوله : ( في التنزل أولا ( لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل وجوده ضروري قلنا ممنوع، نعم لا بد من دليل هو (ضروري) أي معلوم بالضرورة ( وأما وجوده فلا إذ قد لا يكون له أي للدليل ( وجود ) فإن الدليل كما يكون وجودياً يكون عدمياً أيضاً، كعدم الغيم الدال على عدم المطر ( فإنا نستدل بصدق المقدمتين في نفس الأمر على صدق المدلول فيهما ( لا بوجودهما في الخارج على وجود المدلول فيه فإن الدليل والمدلول قد يكونان معاً عدميين والحاصل أنا كما نتوصل بصدق مقدمتي الدليل لا بالعلم بوجودهما إلى المدلول، كذلك نتوصل بتصور أجزاء المعرف لا بالعلم بوجودها إلى الوجود المطلق بالكنه لأنه يكفينا في التصديق المذكور تصور ذلك العارض بالوجه كما يكفينا تصورنا بالوجه.

قوله : ( وليس يلزم إلخ ) جواب عن الثاني بأن النزاع في أن تصور حقيقة الوجود التي هو بها هو بديهي أم لا واللازم مما ذكر أن يكون المفهوم الذي وضع لفظ الوجود له جزءا من مفهوم لفظ وضع وجودي له، فيكون تصور هذا المفهوم مستلزما لتصور ذلك المفهوم، إلا أن يكون حقيقته جزءا من حقيقته فعلى تقدير فرض تصور حقيقة وجودي بالكنه بديهة لا يلزم تصور حقيقة ذلك العارض أصلاً.
لفظ قوله : ( لجواز إلخ) تعليل للنفي المذكور بجواز كون ذينك المفهومين اللذين وضع الوجود ووجودي لهما عارضين لحقيقتهما، فلا يلزم من جزئية المفهوم للمفهوم جزئية الحقيقة للحقيقة هذا ما عندي في حل هذا السؤال والجواب والناظرون في الكتاب بعضهم لم يتعرضوه، وبعضهم قالوا بما لا يرضى بسماعه الآذان الكريمة.
It follows from the fact that the concept of existence is part of the concept of 'existential' that the reality of existence is part of the reality of 'existential', because it is possible that these two concepts are accidental to their reality. (His saying: 'In the first descent, it is necessary to arrive at a proof whose existence is necessary' We said: it is not granted. Yes, a proof is necessary that is (necessary) i.e., known necessarily. (As for its existence, no, for it may not have existence for the proof). For the proof, just as it may be existential, it may also be non-existential, like the absence of clouds indicating the absence of rain. (For we infer from the truth of the two premises in themselves to the truth of the conclusion in them, not from their existence in the external to the existence of the conclusion in it. For the proof and the conclusion may both be non-existential. The result is that just as we arrive at the conclusion through the truth of the premises of the proof, not through knowledge of their existence, similarly we arrive at absolute existence in its essence through conceptualizing the parts of the definition, not through knowledge of their existence, because it suffices for the mentioned assent the conceptualization of that accident in a certain way, just as our conceptualization in a certain way suffices.)

His saying: ('And it does not follow, etc.') is an answer to the second: that the dispute is whether the conceptualization of the reality of existence, which is its very essence, is self-evident or not. What follows from what was mentioned is that the concept for which the word 'existence' was placed is part of the concept of the word placed for 'existential'. So conceptualizing this concept entails conceptualizing that concept, unless its reality is part of its reality. So on the assumption of supposing the conceptualization of the reality of 'existential' in its essence self-evidently, the conceptualization of the reality of that accident does not follow at all.

The wording of his saying: ('Because it is possible, etc.') is a cause for the mentioned negation, by the possibility that those two concepts, for which existence and existential are placed, are accidental to their reality. So it does not follow from the partiality of the concept to the concept the partiality of the reality to the reality. This is what I have in solving this question and answer. The viewers of the book, some of them did not address it, and some said what noble ears are not pleased to hear.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection in the context of defining existence. The objection claims that if the concept of existence is part of the concept of 'existential', then the reality of existence must be part of the reality of 'existential'. The commentator denies this entailment because the two concepts might be accidental (عارضين) to their realities. He then elaborates on the nature of proof: we infer from the truth of premises, not from their external existence, which is analogous to how we conceptualize the parts of a definition to reach absolute existence. He then addresses a second objection: the dispute is about whether the conceptualization of the reality of existence is self-evident, and explains that conceptual entailment does not imply real entailment; the concepts could merely be accidental. He concludes that the common factor in conceptualization is only the concept of 'being' (الكون), and the reality of existence is not conceptualized by anyone, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
الوجودي
العارض
الحقيقة
المفهوم
الصدق
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if a concept is part of another concept, then the corresponding reality must also be part of that reality.
Correction: The commentator argues that concepts can be accidental to their realities, so conceptual parthood does not entail real parthood.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the mere fact that the concept of existence is part of the concept of 'existential' not entail that the reality of existence is part of the reality of 'existential'?
A) Because the two concepts are identical
B) Because the two concepts might be accidental to their realities
C) Because the reality of existence is self-evident
D) Because the proof is not from existence

Key Takeaway
Conceptual parthood does not entail real parthood because concepts can be accidental to their realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you hold about Allah (e.g., His attribute of mercy). How does this lesson challenge the assumption that your conceptual understanding perfectly matches His reality? Will you now hold that concept with more humility, knowing concepts can be accidental?
?
The author argues that we can infer truth without needing existence. In your own life, do you sometimes demand visible evidence before accepting spiritual truths? How does this lesson invite you to trust in conceptual truth even when external evidence is absent?
?
Consider a theological belief you hold firmly (e.g., about predestination). Is it possible that your conceptual understanding has become an 'accidental' layer that doesn't capture the full reality? What would it mean for you to hold that belief with humility, aware that concepts may not entail real parthood?

The Self-Evidence of Existence and Conceptual Entailment
Second objection and response about self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the second objection by clarifying that the dispute is about whether the reality of existence is self-evident, and that conceptual entailment does not require real entailment.

Core Idea
The dispute is about self-evidence, not about conceptual parthood necessitating real parthood.

This passage addresses a second objection concerning the self-evidence of existence. The commentator explains that even if concept A is part of concept B, that does not imply that the reality of A is part of the reality of B, because the concepts might be merely accidental. He concludes by noting the inadequacy of some interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
Read the second part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection is about the self-evidence of the reality of existence. The commentator responds by distinguishing conceptual entailment from real entailment.
2
Understand the key distinction
The commentator says that the entailment is only conceptual: if the concept of existence is part of the concept of 'existential', then conceptualizing the latter entails conceptualizing the former. But this does not mean the reality of existence is part of the reality of 'existential'.
Unless the reality is indeed part, but that is not given.
3
Reflect on the final remarks
The commentator notes that some scholars have not addressed this issue, while others have given unsatisfactory answers. This underscores the nuance of the discussion.

Commentary

?

The Third Argument: Wujud Cannot Be Acquired by Definition or Description
Al-Iji presents the third argument that wujud is self-evident, not acquired by hadd or rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Iji argues that existence (wujud) cannot be acquired through definition (had) or description (rasm), and how he demonstrates the impossibility through analysis of parts.

Core Idea
Existence is so fundamental that no definition or description can capture it; it must be self-evident.

In this passage, al-Iji presents his third argument for the self-evident nature of existence. The argument is directed at those who claim existence is conceivable through its essence but acquired by definition or description. By showing both definition and description fail, al-Iji establishes that existence is a self-evident concept. This lesson unpacks the logic of his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that al-Iji first states the claim and then examines definition.
The argument is dense with commentary; focus on the core logical steps.
2
Identify the two cases
After stating that definition fails because existence is simple, al-Iji considers the possibility of composition. Identify the two cases: parts all existences, and parts all non-existences.
3
Understand the absurdity of each case
In the first case, the part would equal the whole in quiddity—an absurdity. In the second case, an additional existence must arise from the combination, placing composition in the cause, not in existence itself.
Reflect on why these consequences are impossible.
4
Consider the objection and its reply
The passage also addresses an objection about parts being qualified by existence or non-existence. See how the objection is refuted.
This reinforces the argument's conclusion.
5
Conclude the lesson
Understand that the failure of both methods implies existence is not acquired; it must be self-evident (badihi). However, note the argument presupposes the opponent admits existence is conceivable.
This nuance is important.

باطل، وإنما قيدنا به لأن مساواة الأجزاء الخارجية لكلها في الماهية ليس بمحال على الإطلاق، ألا يرى أن طبيعة المياه المتعددة هي بعينها طبيعة الماء الواحد الواقع جزءاً منها، وبالجملة قد تقرر أن كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم البسيط مساو لكله في الاسم والحد كما سيصرح به، نعم الجزء الخارجي لا يساوي كله في الماهية الخارجية أعني الهوية فإن قلت : مقصود المستدل أن أجزاء الوجود إما عين مفهوم الوجود، فيلزم تلك المساواة وهو محال مطلقاً، لأن الجزء داخل في ماهية الكل والشيء ليس داخلاً في نفسه وأيضاً يلزم تقديم الشيء على نفسه قلت: لفظ المساواة يأبى هذه الإرادة كما لا يخفى . قوله : (عارضاً لها إذ لا شك في أنه ليس منفصلاً وأجنبياً عنه بالكلية.
The first observation - first aim: on defining existence and non-existence. (Third) It is only a proof against one who acknowledges that existence is conceived through its essence, and claims that it is acquired: if existence were acquired, then either by definition or description, because the acquirer of conception is limited to these two. Both divisions are false. As for defining it by definition, because definition (as has passed) is only by parts, and existence is simple, so it cannot have a definition. Otherwise, if it is not simple but composite, then its parts are either existences, so the part would be equal to the whole in quiddity, or its parts are not existences but non-existences. Then upon the combination of those parts each of which is not existence, an additional thing must occur which is existence; otherwise there is no existence at all. And that additional thing which occurs upon the combination of the parts is an accident of them, caused by their combination, so they become causes of existence and its subjects, not its parts. Thus composition occurs in the agent of existence or its receiver, not in it. And it could be said: if existence had parts, those parts are qualified either by existence, so the whole is a predicate of the part, or by non-existence, that is false. The commentator says: 'simple' means intellectually and externally, for the mentioned proof, if valid, would indicate negation of composition absolutely. The commentator says: 'his parts' means all or some of them. The meaning of his statement 'first' is universal negation, i.e., none of them is existence, and it cannot be taken as universal affirmation. And his statement 'or not' is a reference to its omission. And it is possible to interpret the first as universal affirmation and the second as universal negation. The existence of the third division, namely that some parts are existences and some are not, is not harmful because it is invalidated by what invalidated the first division. The commentator says: 'the part would be equal to the whole' because when they are assumed to be existences, they agree in existence and differ according to specificities, i.e., differentiate and individuations. So the part is equal to its whole in species-quiddity or genus-quiddity. The equality of the part, insofar as it is a part, to its whole in species-quiddity or genus-quiddity is invalid, because it entails the entrance of the whole into the part, so the part is not a part nor the whole a whole. We only said 'insofar as it is a part' because a simple body like water has its part equal to it in species-quiddity, but not insofar as it is a part but insofar as it is an individual of it, for the part of water is water. From this it is known that specification by the part of the form also is without circularity and is not valid, because knowledge of the universal, if not self-evident in itself but derived from the judgments of each individual, and if the opponent disputes it, we are forced to prove it by the judgments of individuals. Even if we assume the opponent's assistance, it is necessary for it to be knowledge that one observes the premises of his proof, even in summary. If he were to argue for the judgments of its individuals, a circularity would occur. The commentator says: 'the part would be equal to the whole' means the part of the intelligible reality is equal to its whole. That is existences, rather things that are not existences. Then upon the combination of those parts each of which is not existence, an additional thing must occur which is existence; otherwise there is no existence at all. And that additional thing which occurs upon the combination of the parts that is existence is an accident of them, caused by their combination, so they are the causes of existence and its subjects because it is caused by their combination and is an accident of them, not its parts. So composition occurs in the agent of existence or its receiver, not in it, contrary to what is supposed. And it could be said: if existence had parts, those parts are qualified either by existence, so the whole is a predicate of the part, but or by non-existence. That is the intelligible quiddity as a precaution against what was mentioned: specification without a specifier. For the part of external quiddity insofar as it is a part also does not equal its whole in quiddity like hyle and form for body. The commentator says: 'or not' apparently is 'or not existences', but because the disjunction between existences and non-existences is not exhaustive due to the non-exhaustion of concepts in them, the commentator turned the expression from its apparent meaning and interpreted it as 'things that are not existences' i.e., things of which it is true that they are not existences, so that it becomes exhaustive. The commentator says: 'except those parts or the combination which is a relation between those parts' and there is no doubt that it is not existence. The commentator says: 'because it is caused by their combination' so they are causes of it with the condition of combination, since it is not permissible that the combination is a efficient cause because it is a mental consideration. The commentator says: 'an accident of them' so they are its subjects. The commentator says: 'in the agent of existence or its receiver' he used 'or' because composition is in one thing that has considerations, and he did not use 'and' to avoid the misunderstanding that composition occurs in two distinct things. The commentator says: 'either by existence' i.e., absolute existence. The commentator says: 'predicate of the part' i.e., subsisting in it. The commentator says: 'or by non-existence' i.e., by negation of absolute existence, for there is no intermediate between contradictories. That is false, and we only restricted it as such because the equality of external parts to each other in quiddity is not absolutely impossible. Do you not see that the nature of multiple waters is identical to the nature of the single water which is part of them? In summary, it has been established that every part of a simple body is equal to its whole in name and definition, as he will clarify. Yes, the external part is not equal to its whole in external quiddity, i.e., identity. If you say: the intention of the arguer is that the parts of existence are either identical to the concept of existence, so that equality would follow, which is absolutely impossible because the part is inside the quiddity of the whole and a thing is not inside itself, and also it would entail the priority of the thing to itself. I say: the word 'equality' precludes this intention, as is not hidden. The commentator says: 'an accident of them' since there is no doubt that it is not completely separate and alien from it.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (الثالث) for the self-evident nature of existence (الوجود). The argument is directed against one who admits that existence is conceivable through its essence (concept) but claims it is acquired (مكتسب) by definition (حد) or description (رسم). The argument proceeds by showing that both methods fail. First, definition requires parts, but existence is simple (بسيط) in both mind and external reality; hence it cannot have a definition. If existence were composite, its parts would either all be existences—leading to the absurdity that the part equals the whole in quiddity (which violates the definition of part and whole)—or none would be existences, in which case no existence would arise from their combination unless an additional existence occurs, which would then be an accident caused by their combination, placing composition in the cause (agent or receiver) rather than in existence itself. The argument also rejects the possibility of a third division where some parts are existences and some not, as it reduces to the first division's absurdity. The passage further addresses an objection: if existence had parts qualified by existence, the whole would be a predicate of the part, which is also false. The commentator clarifies nuances, such as the distinction between external and intelligible parts, and defends the argument against charges of circularity. Ultimately, the argument establishes that existence cannot be acquired through definition or description, implying it is self-evident (بديهي) for those who grant its conceivability. However, the argument does not apply to those who deny that existence is conceivable at all, since impossibility of definition does not entail self-evident conception—it may simply be inconceivable.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that existence is self-evident for everyone, including those who deny its conceivability.
Correction: The argument only works against those who already admit that existence is conceivable. For those who deny conceivability, the failure of definition does not prove self-evidence; existence might simply be inconceivable.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why can wujud (existence) not be defined by a complete definition (حد)?
A) Because existence is composite and has parts.
B) Because existence is simple and has no parts.
C) Because existence is not conceivable at all.
D) Because definitions are not valid for any concept.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be defined or described because any attempt leads to absurdity, implying it is a self-evident concept for those who think about it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the existence of things is self-evident to you, why do you often live as if God – the ultimate Existence – is far away or abstract? What would change if you truly grasped His existence as directly as you grasp your own?
?
The argument shows that existence cannot be defined or described; it is simple and foundational. How does this challenge your tendency to reduce God to concepts, descriptions, or mental images? Will you now approach Him with more awe, less assumption?
?
Al-Iji's proof only works for those who already admit existence is conceivable. Do you in your heart truly believe that existence is a real, understandable notion, or do you sometimes treat it as a mere word? What must you do to make the reality of existence – and the Necessary Existent – pierce your soul?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
The Three Contradictions of Parts of Existence
Initial argument enumerating three impossible scenarios for parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three initial contradictions that arise if existence were composed of parts.

Core Idea
Existence cannot have parts: each possibility leads to a logical contradiction.

The author begins by stating that a part is not a description of itself. He then presents the hypothetical scenario where existence has parts. Those parts must either be existent (in one of three temporal relations to the whole) or non-existent. This lesson examines the first two existent cases and the non-existent case, showing how each leads to a contradiction, either from priority of parts or from the whole becoming non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial proposition
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the dilemma: if existence had parts, those parts are either characterized by existence (together/after) or not.
2
Identify the three scenarios
Notice that the passage lists three possibilities: parts existent together with the whole, parts existent after the whole, parts non-existent. The case of parts before the whole is not yet addressed.
This lesson covers only the two existent cases (together/after) and the non-existent case.
3
Understand the contradictions
For the existent cases: the part is not prior to the whole, but a part must be prior by definition. For the non-existent case: if parts are non-existent, the whole (existence) would be non-existent.
These contradictions show that existence cannot have parts.

The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-existence. The part is not a description of itself, but is a description of the other parts, so the description in its entirety is not a description, and thus the conjunction of two contradictories is necessitated. And it is said: If existence had parts, then those parts are either characterized by existence together with or after, i.e., together with the existence which is the composite or after it, so the part in terms of its existence is not prior to its whole, but rather it is either together with it or posterior to it. His statement: (conjunction of two contradictories) because there is no doubt that the whole is composed of the part, and that the conjunction of the described thing with something necessitates the conjunction of its description with it, and because the characterization of the part by non-existence necessitates the characterization of the whole which is existence by it, so they combined as the conjunction of the description with the described, and because the occurrence of the parts requires that existence be present, and their being non-existent requires its non-occurrence, so existence is both present and not present. His statement: (those parts) i.e., in that they are parts entering into its constitution. His statement: (the part in terms of its existence is not prior to its whole) while the part in that it is a part must be prior to its whole, and it is assumed that they in that they are parts for it are characterized by existence, and by considering the qualification of 'in that respect' is repelled what the observers were confused about repelling, namely that it is necessary that the part be prior to the whole itself, and the priority of its existence; as for the priority of the existence of the part over the whole itself, it is not necessary, so it is permissible that the existence of the part be posterior to existence itself. His statement: (thus the thing becomes prior, etc.) by necessity that the priority of the individual by which the part is characterized necessitates the priority of the absolute. His statement: (thus the conjunction of two contradictories is necessitated) because the non-existence of the part necessitates the non-existence of the whole which is existence. His statement: (with existence together or after) what is meant by togetherness and posteriority is essential, not temporal, otherwise there is no impossibility in the part not being prior to the whole temporally. And here there is a discussion: that the disjunction is either in relation to togetherness, posteriority, and priority with existence itself or with the existence of existence. On the first, there is no impossibility in the posteriority of the existence of the part from the whole itself; what is established is only the necessity of the priority of the part itself over the whole itself, or the priority of its existence over its existence, not the priority of its existence over the whole itself. On the second, there is no impossibility in the priority of the existence of the part over the existence of the whole, such that two individuals of the quiddity are accidental to its two parts, so that quiddity comes into existence after them by two individuals from them that are accidental to its two parts, and there is no priority of a thing over itself in this. The answer: we choose the second and say: the priority of a thing over itself is necessitated on that estimation, because the attribute which exists in itself exists with its subject according to its existence, i.e., after its existence in itself absolutely. For a white body, the existing whiteness subsists in it, and it is not conceivable to say that non-existing whiteness subsisted in it first and then came into existence. So the existence of the quiddity of existence as assumed is prior in terms of the accidentalness of two individuals from it to its two parts, which necessitates its accidentalness to them. If the characterization of the two parts by existence is assumed before its existence, the priority of the thing over itself is inevitable, so reflect. His statement: (so the part is not prior) And if you say: In them there is another corruption besides what was mentioned, based on the fact that in essential togetherness there is self-differentiation, and in posteriority there is that differentiation with priority as in priority, why did he not address it? I say: (Or) it is characterized by existence (before) i.e., before the existence which is the composite, (so the thing becomes prior) i.e., existence (to itself) or those parts are not characterized by (it) i.e., by existence, so there is no doubt that they are characterized by non-existence, (so existence is pure what has no existence) i.e., those parts that are not characterized by existence. His statement: (so there is no doubt that they, etc.) because there is no intermediate between two contradictories.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the possibility of existence having parts. The author begins with the premise that a part is not a description of itself but of other parts, so the description is incomplete. He then presents an objection: if existence had parts, those parts must be characterized either by existence (together with the composite, after it, or before it) or by non-existence. If the parts are characterized by existence together with or after the composite, then the part is not prior to the whole, but a part must be prior to the whole by virtue of being a part; this leads to contradiction. If the parts are characterized by existence before the composite, then existence would be prior to itself. If the parts are not characterized by existence at all, they are non-existent, so the whole (existence) would be non-existent. In all cases, a contradiction follows. The commentary clarifies that 'togetherness' and 'posteriority' are essential, not temporal, and addresses a subtle objection about the priority of the part's existence over the whole's existence. The author concludes that existence cannot have parts, and thus cannot be defined by composition.

وجود
عدم
جزء
كل
اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that existence could be composed of parts if the parts are existent simultaneously or after the whole, avoiding priority issues.
Correction: The passage shows that even if parts are existent with or after the whole, they still must be prior as parts, leading to contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) The part is prior to itself

C) Existence becomes posterior to non-existence

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot have parts because any assumption about the parts' existence or non-existence leads to a logical contradiction.

?
The part's non-existence would make the whole non-existent. Consider something in your life you treat as indispensable—a possession, a relationship, a status. If that 'part' were taken away, would your ultimate existence before God remain unchanged? What does your attachment reveal about your understanding of existence?

The Case of Parts Prior and the Discussion on Essential Priority
Addresses the third scenario and clarifies essential vs temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining case where parts are existent before the whole, the distinction between essential and temporal priority, and how the author resolves a subtle objection.

Core Idea
If parts are existent before the whole, existence would be prior to itself; the author clarifies essential priority to avoid confusion.

After establishing the basic contradictions, the author turns to the remaining scenario: parts existent before the whole. This seems to avoid the priority issue but leads to a self-priority contradiction. A subtle objection arises concerning the type of priority involved. The author explains the difference between essential and temporal priority, and how the argument still holds when considering essential relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the third scenario
Read the passage that introduces the case where parts are characterized by existence before the whole. Note the resulting contradiction: existence prior to itself.
2
Understand the distinction between essential and temporal priority
The author clarifies that 'togetherness' and 'posteriority' are essential, not temporal. This means priority and posteriority are based on ontological dependence, not time.
This distinction is key to avoiding a misinterpretation of the argument.
3
Follow the answer to the objection
A potential objection is raised: on one reading, priority of the part's existence does not necessarily entail self-priority. The author responds by showing that the assumed scenario inevitably leads to self-priority.
The example of whiteness and body is used to illustrate the necessity of the attribute's existence with its subject.

قوله : ( فليس الجزء بحسب وجوده متقدماً على كله مع أن الجزء من حيث أنه جزء يجب تقدمه على كله وقد فرض أنها من حيث أنها أجزاء له متصفة بالوجود وباعتبار قيد الحيثية اندفع ما تحير في دفعه الناظرون من أن الواجب تقدم الجزء على نفس الكل، وتقدم وجوده وأما تقدم وجود الجزء على نفس الكل، فكلا فيجوز أن يكون وجود الجزء متأخراً عن نفس الوجود .
قوله : ( فيتقدم الشيء إلخ) ضرورة أن تقدم الفرد الذي يتصف به الجزء يستلزم تقدم المطلق .
قوله : ( فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين لأن عدم الجزء يستلزم عدم الكل الذي هو الوجود .
قوله : ( بوجود مع أو بعد المراد بالمعية والبعدية الذاتيتان لا الزمانيتان، وإلا فلا استحالة في عدم تقدم الجزء على الكل زماناً، وهاهنا بحث وهو أن الترديد إما بالنسبة إلى المعية والبعدية والقبلية مع نفس الوجود أو مع وجود الوجود فعلى الأول لا استحالة في تأخر وجود الجزء عن نفس الكل إنما الثابت وجوب تقدم نفس الجزء على نفس الكل أو تقدم وجوده على وجوده لا تقدم وجوده على نفس الكل وعلى الثاني لا استحالة في تقدم وجود الجزء على وجود الكل، بأن يعرض فردان الماهية لجزئيها فتوجد تلك الماهية بعده بفردين منها يعرضان لجزئيها، وليس من في هذا تقدم الشيء على نفسه والجواب أنا نختار الثاني وتقول : يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه علي ذلك التقدير لأن الصفة الموجودة في نفسها توجد بموصوفها باعتبار وجودها أي بعد وجودها في نفسها البتة فإن الجسم الأبيض ما قام به البياض الموجود ولا يعقل أن يقال قام به البياض المعدوم أولاً ثم وجد فوجود ماهية الوجود الموجودة على الفرض مقدم بحسب على عروض فردين منها لجزئيها المستلزم لعروضها لهما، فإذا فرض اتصاف الجزءين بالوجود قبل وجودها تقدم الشيء على نفسه بلا محالة فتأمل.
قوله : ( فليس الجزء متقدماً فإن قلت: فيهما فساد آخر غير ما ذكر بناء على أن في المعية الذات مغايرة الشيء لنفسه وفي البعدية تلك المغايرة مع التقدم كما في القبلية، فلم لم يتعرض له قلت
( أو ) يتصف بوجود (قبل) أي قبل الوجود الذي هو المركب ( فيتقدم الشيء ) أي الوجود ) على نفسه أولا تتصف ( تلك الأجزاء ( به ) أي بالوجود فلا شك أنها تتصف بالعدم ( فالوجود محض ما ليس له وجود ) أعني تلك الأجزاء التي لم تتصف بالوجود
قوله : ( فلاشك أنها إلخ ) لعدم الواسطة بين النقيضين .
The First Observatory - The First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-existence. The part is not a description of itself, but is a description of the other parts, so the description in its entirety is not a description, and thus the conjunction of two contradictories is necessitated. And it is said: If existence had parts, then those parts are either characterized by existence together with or after, i.e., together with the existence which is the composite or after it, so the part in terms of its existence is not prior to its whole, but rather it is either together with it or posterior to it. His statement: (conjunction of two contradictories) because there is no doubt that the whole is composed of the part, and that the conjunction of the described thing with something necessitates the conjunction of its description with it, and because the characterization of the part by non-existence necessitates the characterization of the whole which is existence by it, so they combined as the conjunction of the description with the described, and because the occurrence of the parts requires that existence be present, and their being non-existent requires its non-occurrence, so existence is both present and not present. His statement: (those parts) i.e., in that they are parts entering into its constitution. His statement: (the part in terms of its existence is not prior to its whole) while the part in that it is a part must be prior to its whole, and it is assumed that they in that they are parts for it are characterized by existence, and by considering the qualification of 'in that respect' is repelled what the observers were confused about repelling, namely that it is necessary that the part be prior to the whole itself, and the priority of its existence; as for the priority of the existence of the part over the whole itself, it is not necessary, so it is permissible that the existence of the part be posterior to existence itself. His statement: (thus the thing becomes prior, etc.) by necessity that the priority of the individual by which the part is characterized necessitates the priority of the absolute. His statement: (thus the conjunction of two contradictories is necessitated) because the non-existence of the part necessitates the non-existence of the whole which is existence. His statement: (with existence together or after) what is meant by togetherness and posteriority is essential, not temporal, otherwise there is no impossibility in the part not being prior to the whole temporally. And here there is a discussion: that the disjunction is either in relation to togetherness, posteriority, and priority with existence itself or with the existence of existence. On the first, there is no impossibility in the posteriority of the existence of the part from the whole itself; what is established is only the necessity of the priority of the part itself over the whole itself, or the priority of its existence over its existence, not the priority of its existence over the whole itself. On the second, there is no impossibility in the priority of the existence of the part over the existence of the whole, such that two individuals of the quiddity are accidental to its two parts, so that quiddity comes into existence after them by two individuals from them that are accidental to its two parts, and there is no priority of a thing over itself in this. The answer: we choose the second and say: the priority of a thing over itself is necessitated on that estimation, because the attribute which exists in itself exists with its subject according to its existence, i.e., after its existence in itself absolutely. For a white body, the existing whiteness subsists in it, and it is not conceivable to say that non-existing whiteness subsisted in it first and then came into existence. So the existence of the quiddity of existence as assumed is prior in terms of the accidentalness of two individuals from it to its two parts, which necessitates its accidentalness to them. If the characterization of the two parts by existence is assumed before its existence, the priority of the thing over itself is inevitable, so reflect. His statement: (so the part is not prior) And if you say: In them there is another corruption besides what was mentioned, based on the fact that in essential togetherness there is self-differentiation, and in posteriority there is that differentiation with priority as in priority, why did he not address it? I say: (Or) it is characterized by existence (before) i.e., before the existence which is the composite, (so the thing becomes prior) i.e., existence (to itself) or those parts are not characterized by (it) i.e., by existence, so there is no doubt that they are characterized by non-existence, (so existence is pure what has no existence) i.e., those parts that are not characterized by existence. His statement: (so there is no doubt that they, etc.) because there is no intermediate between two contradictories.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the possibility of existence having parts. The author begins with the premise that a part is not a description of itself but of other parts, so the description is incomplete. He then presents an objection: if existence had parts, those parts must be characterized either by existence (together with the composite, after it, or before it) or by non-existence. If the parts are characterized by existence together with or after the composite, then the part is not prior to the whole, but a part must be prior to the whole by virtue of being a part; this leads to contradiction. If the parts are characterized by existence before the composite, then existence would be prior to itself. If the parts are not characterized by existence at all, they are non-existent, so the whole (existence) would be non-existent. In all cases, a contradiction follows. The commentary clarifies that 'togetherness' and 'posteriority' are essential, not temporal, and addresses a subtle objection about the priority of the part's existence over the whole's existence. The author concludes that existence cannot have parts, and thus cannot be defined by composition.

معية ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if parts are existent before the whole, the whole is not prior to itself because the parts and whole are distinct entities.
Correction: The author shows that since the parts are constitutive of existence, the whole's existence is dependent on the parts; if the parts exist before the whole, then existence exists before itself, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence if parts are characterized by existence before the whole?
A) The whole becomes non-existent
B) Existence is prior to itself
C) No contradiction arises
D) The parts become identical to the whole

Key Takeaway
Even when considering essential priority, the case of parts before the whole leads to self-priority, confirming that existence cannot have parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often think of your actions as 'parts' that make up the 'whole' of your character. But this lesson shows that no part can be truly prior to the whole without contradiction. Does this mean your actions don't really compose your character? How does this change the way you see your spiritual growth—as something that emerges from a whole that already exists?
?
The argument forces you to confront the fact that existence cannot have parts that precede it. Consider your own existence: are you trying to add 'parts' (good deeds, knowledge) to build a self that is already complete in Allah's knowledge? What if the priority is not in time but in essence? How does that reframe your efforts today?
?
If the attribute must exist with its subject (like whiteness with a white body), then your faith cannot be added piece by piece; it must be a whole that characterizes you. Is there an area where you are treating your faith as a collection of parts to be assembled later, rather than a single existence that should define you now? What will you do about it today?

Why Existence Cannot Be Defined by Form (al-Rasm)
The author states two reasons why existence cannot be defined by form (al-rasm).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why existence cannot be defined through form (al-rasm), and why the condition of being 'more known' makes definition by form impossible for the most fundamental concept.

Core Idea
Existence is so fundamental that no definition can capture it.

In the previous lesson, we saw that defining existence by limit (al-hadd) fails because existence has no genus or differentia. Now the author turns to definition by form (al-rasm) and gives two compelling reasons why it too fails. As you read, notice how the argument hinges on the fact that existence is the most known and most general concept, making it indefinable by anything else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the translation to grasp the author's two reasons.
The passage includes both the original text and a commentary (sharh) that clarifies key points.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage explicitly states two reasons (face 1 and face 2). Find each one and note how they are introduced.
The first reason concerns the nature of form (rasm) failing to convey essence; the second concerns the requirement of being 'more known' (a'raf).
3
Understand the commentary
The commentary explains why this reasoning does not apply to definition by limit (hadd), and clarifies that 'more known' means prior in knowledge.
The commentary also addresses the apparent problem that existence might be equal in knowledge to something else.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these arguments show that existence is indefinable by any method. This reinforces the primacy of existence in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعريفه بالرسم فلوجهين أحدهما أن الرسم لا يفيد معرفة كنه الحقيقة والنزاع فيه لا في وجه يمكن استفادته من الرسم الثاني أن الرسم يجب أن يكون بالأعرف في شرائط المعرف ولا أعرف من الوجود بالاستقراء فإنا تتبعنا المفهومات فوجدنا الوجود أعرف من كل ما نحاول تعريفه به وأيضاً فهو أي الوجود أعم المفهومات
قوله : (بالأعرف أي بما هو أقدم معرفة، وحينئذ يظهر أنه لا يجري هذا الوجه في امتناع التحديد لأن الأجزاء تتقدم معرفتها على معرفة المحدود قطعاً، ومن هذا ظهر أن اشتراط الأعرفية في مطلق المعرف إنما هو بالنظر إلى بعض أفراده .
قوله : ( أعرف إلخ) فنفي الأعرفية في المتن إما كناية عن إثبات الأعرفية كما هو المتفاهم في العرف بناء على أن المساواة قلما تتحقق بين الشيئين فهي كالمعدوم، وإما اكتفاء على ما هو المقصود فإنه إذا لم يكن أعرف منه مفهوم امتنع رسمه وإن وجد ما يساويه بناء على أن شرطه الأعرفية .
As for defining it by form (al-rasm), it is [impossible] for two reasons: one is that the form does not provide knowledge of the essence of the reality, and the dispute about it is not in a way that can be derived from the form. The second is that the form must be by [what is] more known (al-a'raf) in the conditions of the definiens, and nothing is more known than existence by induction, for we examined the concepts and found existence more known than everything we try to define by it. And also, it, i.e., existence, is the most general of concepts. His statement: (by what is more known) i.e., by what is prior in knowledge. And then it becomes apparent that this aspect does not apply to the impossibility of definition by limits (al-hadd), because the parts are necessarily prior in knowledge to the knowledge of the defined. And from this it appears that the condition of being more known in the absolute definiens is only with respect to some of its individuals. His statement: (more known, etc.) So the negation of being more known in the text is either a metonymy for affirming being more known, as is understood in custom, based on the fact that equality rarely occurs between two things, so it is like non-existent, or it is sufficient for what is intended, because if there is no concept more known than it, its form is impossible, even if there exists something equal to it, based on the condition that it be more known.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents two reasons why existence cannot be defined by form (al-rasm). The first reason is that form does not give knowledge of the essence; the dispute about existence is not about something that can be derived from form. The second reason is that form must employ something more known (a'raf) than the defined term, but existence is the most known concept by induction. Additionally, existence is the most general concept. The commentary clarifies that 'more known' means prior in knowledge, and notes that this argument does not apply to definition by limits (al-hadd) because parts of the limit are always more known. The commentary also explains that the negation of 'more known' in the text is either a figurative affirmation or sufficient to show impossibility.

استقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that definition by form (al-rasm) is always possible for any concept.
Correction: This passage shows that definition by form fails for existence because existence is the most known and most general concept, making it impossible to find a more known or equally known definiens.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason why existence cannot be defined by form (al-rasm)?
A) Form does not provide knowledge of the essence
B) Form requires something less known
C) Existence has no definition
D) Form is not a valid type of definition

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be defined by form because form does not reveal essence, and no concept is more known than existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is the most known concept, why do you still sometimes feel disconnected from your own existence or struggle to find meaning in it? What does this reveal about your reliance on definitions versus direct awareness?
?
This lesson argues that form cannot define existence because it doesn't touch the essence. When you describe yourself—your identity, your purpose—do you rely on forms (roles, labels) that miss your essence? What would it take to live from your essence rather than from descriptions?
?
The condition of being more known makes definition by form impossible for existence. In your pursuit of knowledge about God, tawheed, or the unseen, have you ever tried to 'define' these realities in a way that actually reduces them? How can you guard against this intellectual trap?
