الوجود الذهني، كلوازم الماهية ووجه الاندفاع أن العوارض الذهنية ليس لها اختصاص بماهية واحدة، بل كل منها شامل لماهيات كثيرة لا يعد في العرف من خواص واحد منها وأما حديث لوازم الماهية فاندفع بقيد الجميع، إذ بعض الآثار وإن ترتب على الوجود الذهني، وهو لوازم الماهية فجميعها لا يترتب إلا على الوجود الخارجي.
The first observatory - the fourth objective: On mental existence. There is no doubt that fire, for example, has an existence by which its judgments appear and its effects issue, such as illumination and burning and others, and this existence is called concrete and external and original, and this is indisputable. The dispute is whether fire, besides that existence, has another existence by which those judgments and effects do not follow, and this other existence is called mental and shadowy. The nature of existence, even if specific, is such that it may be among the concomitants of its individuals, because abstraction and self-subsistence are prior to individuation, so it cannot be caused by it. For saying: (But it is an accidental matter etc.): due to the difference in reality, it is possible that some require increase and others abstraction. For saying: (Its judgments etc.): i.e., the judgments known to be established for it and the effects desired from it for everyone, as indicated by his saying 'no doubt' and 'this is indisputable', and the explanation by his saying 'from illumination and burning', and in his saying 'appear and issue' there is an indication that what is meant by judgments is what is not an agent for it, and by effects what is an agent for it. For saying: (Concrete): i.e., attributed to the thing itself because it is the existence of the thing in itself, unlike mental existence, for it is the existence of its form. And his saying 'original' i.e., having a root and origin, not a shadow and representation of something. For saying: (Concerning fire): let no one imagine from the mention of fire that the dispute is about mental existence of external existences, for it is merely for illustration. For saying: (Those judgments and effects, whether other judgments and effects follow from it or not. And by what we have set down for you in explaining the meaning of external and mental existence, what has been said is refuted: if external effects are intended, a circle occurs; if general is intended, mental existence enters, for it is also a source of secondary intelligibles. And there is no need for what has been said: that there are no judgments or effects for mental existence, and secondary intelligibles are effects of personal forms subsisting in the mind, and they are from external existences; nor for what has been said: that the meaning is that it is an agent of effects, while mental existence is not an agent; nor for what has been said: that what is meant is specific effects, while mental effects are common among mental existences. For saying: (Its judgments appear and its effects issue): what is meant by the judgments and effects of fire is all that is specific to it, so what is said is refuted: that the distinction between the two existences by what is mentioned is not clear, since just as effects and judgments follow from concrete existence, so also from shadowy existence follow such as universality, particularity, genericity, specific difference, etc. Rather, some of what follows from external existence follows from mental existence, such as the concomitants of quiddity. And the way of refuting is that mental accidents do not have specificity to one quiddity, but each includes many quiddities not counted in custom as properties of one of them. As for the talk of the concomitants of quiddity, it is refuted by the restriction of 'all', for although some effects follow from mental existence, namely the concomitants of quiddity, all of them do not follow except on external existence.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage defines mental existence and distinguishes it from external existence. It begins by affirming that fire has an external existence with characteristic effects (illumination, burning). The dispute is whether it also has a mental existence without those effects. Mental existence is then defined as a shadowy existence. The commentary clarifies that the nature of existence may have different concomitants, and that judgments and effects refer to what is specific to the thing. It also explains that 'concrete' means existence in itself, while mental existence is existence of the form. The text then addresses several objections: the distinction between the two existences is not clear because mental existence also has effects like universals; but the answer is that mental accidents are not specific to one quiddity, and the concomitants of quiddity do not all follow from mental existence. The passage concludes that all specific effects follow only from external existence.

What Is Kalam?

Proof of Mental Existence from True Judgments
Proof that mental existence is required for true positive judgments about non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni kalam proves the reality of mental existence by analyzing the implications of true positive judgments about non-existent things.

Core Idea
True judgment requires its subject to exist somewhere — if not externally, then mentally.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a key argument for the existence of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The commentator demonstrates that we make meaningful, true statements about things that have no external reality — like 'impossible' or 'general possibility' — and that such statements presuppose some mode of existence for those subjects. Since they clearly do not exist outside, they must exist in the mind. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently to get its rhythm, then read the literal English translation. Notice the density of the argument.
The Arabic is compact; the translation helps clarify.
2
Identify the examples given
The author lists several true positive judgments about things that do not exist externally. Find each example and notice how they illustrate the premise.
Examples include 'subject to general possibility', 'the impossible is more specific than the non-existent', etc.
3
Understand the key principle
The sentence 'thubut shay' li-ghayrihi far' thubutihi fi nafsih' is central: the existence of something for another depends on its own existence. Grasp this principle.
This is a logical axiom in kalam.
4
Trace the logical flow
Follow the argument: premise (we judge non-existents truly) → principle (judgment requires subject existence) → conclusion (since not external, mental).
The conclusion is 'wa huwa al-matlab' — that is what is sought.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is foundational for discussions of mental existence and how it relates to other topics in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحكم عليه أي على ما لا وجود له في الخارج بأحكام ثبوتية صادقة ككونها محكوماً عليها بالإمكان العام وملزومة، أو لازمة لبعض الأشياء وكون الممتنع مثلاً أخص من المعدوم وأعم من شريك الباري وكونه متعقلاً، إلى غير ذلك من الأحكام الإيجابية الصادقة في نفس الأمر، سواء كانت صادقة على مفهوم الممتنع أو على ما صدق عليه وإنه أي الحكم على تلك الأمور المتصورة بأحكام ثبوتية صادقة يستدعي ثبوتها إذ ثبوت الشيء لغيره في نفس الأمر فرع ثبوته في نفسه وإذ ليس ثبوت تلك الأمور المتصورة في الخارج فهو في الذهن وهو المطلوب.
And we judge it — i.e., what has no existence in the external world — with true positive judgments, such as its being subject to general possibility, and being necessary for something, or being a necessary concomitant of something, and that the impossible (al-mumtani‘) is more specific than the non-existent and more general than the associate of God, and its being conceivable, and other true affirmative judgments in reality, whether they are true of the concept of the impossible or of what it applies to. And that — i.e., judging those conceived things with true positive judgments — requires their existence, because the existence of something for another in reality is a branch of its existence in itself; and since those conceived things do not exist externally, they exist in the mind — and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument for the reality of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) in Sunni kalam. The commentator begins by stating a premise: we make true positive judgments about things that have no external existence. Examples include predicating general possibility, being a necessary condition or consequent, and the relationship of 'impossible' to 'non-existent' and 'associate of God'. These judgments are true in themselves (fi nafs al-amr). Since they are true, the subjects of these judgments must have some kind of existence; otherwise, the predication would be empty. But if they have no external existence, they must exist mentally. The argument is that true predication requires the subject to exist in some mode, and the only mode left is mental existence. The commentator also clarifies that the predicates themselves are positive attributes (ahkam thubutiyya), not the negative, and he explains that the examples given are instances of the predicate (mahmul), not the judgment itself.

أحكام ثبوتية
محكوم عليه
ممتنع
إمكان عام
فرعية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence is the same as external existence.
Correction: Mental existence is a distinct conceptual mode, not a physical or external reality.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator use as evidence for the existence of mental existence?
A) The impossibility of external existence.
B) True positive judgments about things with no external existence.
C) The concept of the associate of God.
D) The need for a subject in a proposition.

Key Takeaway
True judgments about non-existent things prove they have mental existence.

?

Defining Mental Existence by Essence and Identity
Defines mental existence as distinction by essence only, not by identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for distinguishing mental existence from external existence: that mental existence is distinguished by essence alone, while external existence is distinguished by identity.

Core Idea
Mental existence is not a separate world; it is a distinction in essence without identity.

The author begins by reiterating the foundational distinction between identity (huwiyya) and essence (māhiyya). This distinction allows him to define mental existence precisely: it is what is imprinted in the mind and distinguished only by its essence, not by any real identity. In this lesson, we will unpack this definition and see how it avoids the problem of impossible objects existing externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first paragraph of the source Arabic carefully, noting the two branches.
Notice that the author uses the term 'imprinted in absent things' (المرتسم في الأمور الغائبة).
2
Identify the two types of distinction
The passage contrasts two ways something can be distinguished: by identity (الهوية) or by essence (الماهية) only.
3
Understand the consequence
If distinguished by identity, the impossible would exist externally; if by essence only, it is mental existence.
This avoids the absurdity of impossible things having external existence.
4
Consider the alternative formulation
The author restates the condition in terms of judgments and effects being entailed.
This reinforces the same criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إن كانت الهويات إلخ) هذا مبني على ما سبق من أن ما انحاز بالهوية فهو موجود خارجي، وما انحاز بالماهية فقط فهو موجود ذهني، فالمرتسم في الأمور الغائبة إن انحاز بالهوية بهذا الارتسام فهو موجود خارجي، فيلزم وجود الممتنع في الخارج، وإن انحاز بالماهية فقط فهو موجود ذهني، إذ لا نعني بالموجود الذهني إلا هذا. وبعبارة أخرى إن المرتسم فيها إن ترتب عليه أحكامها وآثارها بهذا الارتسام يلزم تحقق الممتنع في الخارج، وإن لم يترتب عليها تلك الأحكام والآثار فهو موجود ذهني.
قوله: (وإنما لم يتعرض إلخ) يعني كان المنع مستنداً بسندين، فإبطال أحد السندين لا يجدي في دفع المنع، فأجاب: بأن بطلانه لما كان ظاهراً لم يتعرض له، وذلك لأن القول بقيام الممتنعات بذواتها في الخارج أظهر بطلاناً من القول بقيامها بالغير في الخارج.
قوله: (والحاصل إلخ) أي حاصل الاستدلال بعد ملاحظة ما ذكره المصنف في دفع منع الإمام، وهو بطلان أحد الشقين واستلزام الشق الآخر للمطلوب فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه بعض الناظرين.
His saying: (If the identities, etc.) This is based on what preceded: that what is distinguished by identity is external existence, and what is distinguished by essence only is mental existence. So what is imprinted in absent things—if it is distinguished by identity through this imprinting, it is external existence, and then the existence of the impossible in the external world follows; but if it is distinguished by essence only, it is mental existence, for we mean by mental existence nothing other than this. In other words, if what is imprinted therein has its judgments and effects entailed by this imprinting, then the realization of the impossible in the external world follows; but if those judgments and effects are not entailed, then it is mental existence.
His saying: (And he only did not address, etc.) means the denial (al-manʿ) was based on two chains; invalidating one chain does not help in repelling the denial. So he answered: because its falsity was apparent, he did not address it. That is because the claim of the self-subsistence of impossible things in the external world is more obviously false than the claim of their subsistence in another.
His saying: (And the conclusion, etc.) i.e., the conclusion of the inference after considering what the author mentioned in repelling the Imam's denial: it is the falsity of one of the two branches and the entailment of the other branch of what is sought. So reflect, for it is one of the things in which some observers slipped.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of mental existence by distinguishing it from external existence. The commentary explains the matn: the distinction is based on what was said earlier—that what is distinguished by identity (huwiyya) is external existence, and what is distinguished only by essence (māhiyya) is mental existence. Thus, if the imprint in absent things (like the active intellect) is distinguished by identity through that imprinting, it would be external existence, leading to the impossible being actualized externally; but if distinguished by essence only, it is mental existence. The commentator clarifies this with an alternative formulation: if the imprinted thing has its judgments (aḥkām) and effects entailed by that imprinting, then the impossible would be realized externally; if not, it is mental existence. Then the commentator explains why the author did not address the possibility of self-subsistence of impossible things: because the denial was based on two chains, and invalidating one chain is insufficient. The author avoided addressing it because its falsity is obvious: claiming impossible things subsist by themselves in the external is more clearly false than claiming they subsist in another. Finally, the commentator summarizes: the inference results in the falsity of one branch and the entailment of the other for the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
الهوية
الماهية
الوجود الخارجي
الوجود الذهني
الممتنع
التميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence is a separate realm of concrete objects.
Correction: Mental existence is not a separate concrete realm; it is merely a distinction in essence without external identity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of mental existence?
A) It is distinguished by identity
B) It is distinguished by essence only
C) It entails judgments and effects
D) It exists in the external world

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is defined by being distinguished solely by essence, without external identity or effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine or conceive of the afterlife or the angels, do you treat them as mere mental constructs without identity, or do you actively affirm their external reality with identity? How does this distinction affect your sense of accountability?
?
If mental existence is only distinguished by essence and does not entail external effects, how can your mental knowledge of God (e.g., His attributes) translate into actions that reflect His external reality? What is the practical implication of this for your worship?
?
This lesson shows that if a mental concept were given identity, it would become external existence—even for impossible things. How does this guard you against confusing your subjective imagination with objective truth? In what area of your life might you be treating a mere mental image as if it had external identity, and what would it mean to correct that?

Refuting an Objection to the Definition of Mental Existence
Explains why the author did not address one chain of the objection and summarizes the inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author did not address a particular chain of denial and how the conclusion of the inference is reached.

Core Idea
Invalidating one chain of a multi-chain denial does not refute the whole objection; the author only addressed the obviously false chain.

After defining mental existence, the author anticipates an objection (manʿ) based on two chains. He explains that he did not explicitly address one chain because its falsehood is self-evident. In this lesson, we will examine this response and the final conclusion of the inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text on the two chains
Read the second and third paragraphs of the source Arabic, which discuss the denial's two chains and the conclusion.
2
Understand why invalidating one chain is insufficient
The author states that the denial was based on two chains; invalidating one does not remove the denial entirely.
This is a logical point: a multi-premise objection requires refuting each premise.
3
Grasp why the author left one chain unaddressed
He did not address it because its falsehood is obvious: claiming impossible things subsist by themselves in the external world is even more absurd than claiming they subsist in another (i.e., mentally).
This is a practical rhetorical decision, not a logical gap.
4
Read the concluding summary and reflect
The conclusion states that one of the two branches (identity-based distinction) is false, and the other (essence-based) entails the desired conclusion. The commentator warns that some have slipped here.
Careful reflection is needed to avoid misunderstanding.

The Proof for Mental Existence: Avoiding Sophistry
Initial dilemma proving mental existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dilemma that proves mental existence and how it avoids the charge of sophistry.

Core Idea
Mental existence is proven by the impossibility of impossible things existing externally.

This passage begins the proof for mental existence by presenting a dilemma: if what we conceive had to exist externally, then impossible things would exist, which is absurd. The alternative is that they exist mentally. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage from 'الهويات' to 'الوجود الذهني' along with its translation. Notice the structure: a dilemma is presented.
The argument uses a conditional phrase 'لزم'.
2
Identify the two horns
Identify the first possibility: external identity leading to sophistry. The second possibility: mental existence. Understand why the first is rejected and the second is accepted.
The author uses 'وإن كان' to introduce the alternative.
3
Reflect on the necessity of mental existence
Why does mental existence not lead to sophistry? Because it does not assert external existence for impossible things.
This is the key move in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهويات أي هويات ما نتصوره لزم تحقق هوية الممتنع في الخارج وأنه سفسطة ظاهرة البطلان وإن كان المرتسم فيها هو الصور والماهيات الكلية، فهو المراد بالوجود الذهني إذ غرضنا ومقصودنا إثبات نوع من التميز للمعقولات التي هي الماهيات الكلية هو غير التميز بالهوية الذي نسميه بالوجود الخارجي سواء اخترعها الذهن أي اخترع الذهن تلك المعقولات فيكون ذلك النوع من التميز لها في ذهننا أو لاحظها أي لاحظ الذهن تلك المعقولات من موضع آخر كالعقل الفعال فيكون ذلك النوع من التميز لها فيه وإنما لم يتعرض لقيام ما نتصوره بنفسه، لأن بطلانه أظهر والحاصل أن تلك الأمور المتصورة إذا كانت ممتنعة الوجود في الخارج، لم يمكن أن يكون لها وجود أصيل لا قائم بنفسها، ولا بغيرها فوجب أن يكون لها وجود ظلي في قوة دراكة سواء كانت هي النفس الناطقة أو غيرها، وهو المطلوب
The identities — meaning the identities of what we conceive — necessitate the realization of the identity of the impossible in the external world, and that [leads] to sophistry, which is clearly false. But if what is imprinted [in the mind] is the forms and universal essences, then that is what is meant by mental existence, because our purpose and aim is to prove a type of distinction for the intelligibles, which are the universal essences, that is other than the distinction by identity which we call external existence. Whether the mind invents them — i.e., the mind invents those intelligibles, so that type of distinction belongs to them in our mind — or it observes them — i.e., the mind observes those intelligibles from another place, such as the Active Intellect, so that type of distinction belongs to them therein. And he did not address the self-subsistence of what we conceive because its falsehood is more apparent. The summary: when those conceived things are impossible of existence in the external world, they cannot have a real existence, neither self-subsistent nor subsisting in another, so necessarily they have a shadow existence in a perceiving power, whether it be the rational soul or something else. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and replies to an objection from the Imam. The author begins by stating that if the identities we conceive were to have an external identity, this would require the impossible to exist externally, which is sophistry. But if what is imprinted in the mind is the forms and universal essences, then that is mental existence. The aim is to prove a type of distinction for intelligibles different from external existence. Whether the mind invents these intelligibles or observes them from elsewhere (like the Active Intellect), the mental existence is still valid. The author notes that the objection about self-subsistence of concepts is not addressed because it is too obviously false. The summary: impossible things cannot have real existence, so they must have a shadow existence in a perceiving power, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
الهويات
الممتنع
سفسطة
الوجود الذهني
الصور
الماهيات الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if the identities of conceived things were to have external existence?
A) They would be impossible.
B) That would entail the existence of the impossible, leading to sophistry.
C) They would be mental.
D) They would be self-subsistent.

Key Takeaway
The proof for mental existence begins by eliminating external existence for impossible concepts, leading to mental existence as the only coherent option.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture an impossible object in your mind, do you unconsciously assume it has some shadowy external reality? How does the proof for mental existence force you to admit that only its form exists in your mind, not the thing itself? Will you now examine other concepts you hold to see if you mistake mental images for external facts?
?
Have you ever debated someone who argued that because you can conceive of something (like an injustice or a perfect being), it must exist somewhere? How does knowing about sophistry (safsata) protect you from falling into that error in your own thinking?
?
The passage says mental existence consists of 'forms and universal essences.' Think of a concept you struggle with—like 'justice' or 'God.' Do you treat it as a mere mental construct, or do you believe it points to something beyond the mind? What would it mean for your life if you truly accepted that your most abstract thoughts have only mental existence?

The Nature of Mental Existence: Invention or Observation?
Explanation of mental existence types and conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possibilities for how intelligibles exist in the mind and the conclusion that they have a shadow existence.

Core Idea
Universal essences have a shadow existence in a perceiving power, whether invented by the mind or observed from elsewhere.

After establishing mental existence, the author clarifies that mental existence means a distinction for intelligibles other than external identity. Whether the mind invents them or observes them from the Active Intellect, they still have a shadow existence. This lesson explores that explanation and the final conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended explanation
Read the passage from 'إذ غرضنا' to 'وهو المطلوب'. Understand the purpose of proving a distinction for intelligibles.
The author introduces two scenarios for how mental existence occurs.
2
Explore the two scenarios
Compare the mind inventing the intelligibles vs. observing them from the Active Intellect. In both cases, they have a mental existence.
The phrase 'سواء اخترعها...أو لاحظها' shows it doesn't matter which.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The summary states that impossible things cannot have real existence, so they must have a shadow existence in a perceiving power. This is the desired conclusion.
The term 'وجود ظلي' is crucial.

This passage presents the proof for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and replies to an objection from the Imam. The Problem of the Non-Existent: Introduction and Significance
Introduces the sixth objective and its significance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological problem of whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing (shay'), why it is considered a major question (ummahat al-masa'il), and how it relates to the issue of existence being additional to essence.

Core Idea
The status of the non-existent as a 'thing' is a foundational issue in kalam.

This lesson introduces the sixth theological objective: whether the non-existent is a thing. The author begins by stating that this is a major question because many rulings depend on it, such as the question of whether essences are created. He then cites Imam al-Razi, who links this issue to the debate on whether existence is additional to essence. An objection is presented, and the author urges careful reflection on the actual locus of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: statement, importance, example, quote, objection, conclusion.
2
Identify why this issue is important
The passage says it is among the 'major theological questions' because many rulings branch from it. Find the example given: the issue of whether essences are created.
The phrase 'ummahat al-masa'il' indicates its foundational nature.
3
Understand al-Razi's connection
Imam al-Razi links this problem to the issue of whether existence is additional to essence. Note how the two positions (addition vs. identity) affect the possibility of the non-existent being a thing.
This connects to broader debates in kalam and falsafa.
4
Consider the objection and reflection
Read the objection that reverses the reasoning and the author's comment that all confusion arises from failing to reflect on the locus of dispute. Reflect on what 'mahall al-niza' might mean.
The author is warning against misunderstanding the precise point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا] في أن المعدوم شيء أم لا وإنها من أمهات المسائل الكلامية إذ يتفرع عليها أحكام كثيرة من جملتها أن الماهيات غير مجعولة، وسيرد عليك بعضها عن قريب قال الإمام الرازي: المسألة متفرعة على القول بزيادة الوجود على الماهية فإن القائل باتحادهما لا يمكنه القول بها، قيل ويمكن أن يعكس الحكم في عدم التمايز لعدم الثبوت أصلاً، لأنه إذا كان التمايز باعتبار كونها موجودة في الذهن لم يكن تمايزها من حيث إنها معدومات والكلام فيه ولأن الكلام في تمايز المعدومات مطلقاً لا تمايز المعدومات الخارجية فتدبر، فإن كل ذلك منشؤه عدم التدبر لمحل النزاع.
[The Sixth Objective: Is the non-existent a thing or not?] Concerning whether the non-existent is a thing or not, and that it is among the major theological questions, for many rulings branch from it, among them that essences are not made (ghayr maj'ula), and some of them will come to you shortly. Imam al-Razi said: The question branches from the statement that existence is additional to essence, for he who says they are identical cannot hold it. It was said: It is possible to reverse the ruling in the denial of distinctness due to lack of establishment at all, because if distinctness is by virtue of their being existent in the mind, then their distinctness is not insofar as they are non-existents—and the discussion is about that—and because the discussion is about the distinctness of non-existents absolutely, not the distinctness of external non-existents. So reflect, for all of that arises from lack of reflection on the locus of dispute.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing (shay') or not. It begins by stating that this is a major theological question (ummahat al-masa'il) because many rulings depend on it, such as the question of whether essences (mahiyyat) are created (maj'ula) or not. Then it quotes Imam al-Razi, who says that the problem is derivative of the issue of whether existence is additional to essence: one who believes existence and essence are identical cannot hold that the non-existent is a thing. The passage then presents an objection: one could reverse the reasoning, arguing that if distinctness (tamayyuz) among non-existents is due to their existence in the mind, then they are not distinct as non-existents. The author comments that all such confusion arises from failing to properly understand the locus of the dispute (mahall al-niza').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the problem only concerns whether non-existent things have some kind of external reality.
Correction: The problem actually concerns whether the non-existent can be considered a 'thing' (shay') in itself, which has implications for the ontological status of essences and their distinctness.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, the question of whether the non-existent is a thing depends on which prior issue?
A) The issue of whether essences are created
B) The issue of whether existence is additional to essence
C) The issue of whether non-existents are distinct
D) The issue of whether the intellect can perceive non-existents

Key Takeaway
The problem of whether the non-existent is a thing is a foundational theological question linked to the relationship between existence and essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult abstract theological question like this, what is your first reaction? Do you avoid it, dismiss it as irrelevant, or engage deeply? The passage warns that confusion arises from lack of reflection. Is there an area of your faith where you have avoided thinking deeply? What step will you take this week to confront it?
?
The objector in the passage tried to reverse the argument based on mental existence, but the author says this arises from not reflecting on the true locus of dispute. Have you ever held a belief about God or His attributes without truly understanding its foundations? Are you willing to re-examine that belief, even if it feels uncomfortable, to ensure it is built on solid knowledge? If so, what specific teaching will you investigate?
